<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Number theory</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Mathematics of integer properties</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the book by André Weil, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Number_Theory:_An_Approach_through_History_from_Hammurapi_to_Legendre" title="Number Theory: An Approach through History from Hammurapi to Legendre">Number Theory: An Approach through History from Hammurapi to Legendre</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Numerology" title="Numerology">Numerology</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:251px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spirale_Ulam_150.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="752" data-file-width="750" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Spirale_Ulam_150.jpg/249px-Spirale_Ulam_150.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Spirale_Ulam_150.jpg/374px-Spirale_Ulam_150.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Spirale_Ulam_150.jpg/499px-Spirale_Ulam_150.jpg 2x" width="249"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spirale_Ulam_150.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The distribution of <a href="/wiki/Prime_number" title="Prime number">prime numbers</a> is a central point of study in number theory. This <a href="/wiki/Ulam_spiral" title="Ulam spiral">Ulam spiral</a> serves to illustrate it, hinting, in particular, at the conditional <a href="/wiki/Independence_(probability_theory)" title="Independence (probability theory)">independence</a> between being prime and being a value of certain quadratic polynomials.</div></div></div>

</tr><tr><th class="sidebar-heading">
<a href="/wiki/Portal:Mathematics" title="Portal:Mathematics">Portal</a></th></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Math_topics_TOC" title="Template:Math topics TOC"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Math_topics_TOC" title="Template talk:Math topics TOC"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Math_topics_TOC&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Number theory</b> (or <b>arithmetic</b> or <b>higher arithmetic</b> in older usage) is a branch of <a href="/wiki/Pure_mathematics" title="Pure mathematics">pure mathematics</a> devoted primarily to the study of the <a href="/wiki/Integer" title="Integer">integers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arithmetic_function" title="Arithmetic function">integer-valued functions</a>. German mathematician <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss" title="Carl Friedrich Gauss">Carl Friedrich Gauss</a> (1777–1855) said, "Mathematics is the queen of the sciences—and number theory is the queen of mathematics."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong19721_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong19721-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[note 1]</a></sup> Number theorists study <a href="/wiki/Prime_number" title="Prime number">prime numbers</a> as well as the properties of <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_object" title="Mathematical object">mathematical objects</a> made out of integers (for example, <a href="/wiki/Rational_number" title="Rational number">rational numbers</a>) or defined as generalizations of the integers (for example, <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_integer" title="Algebraic integer">algebraic integers</a>).
</p><p>Integers can be considered either in themselves or as solutions to equations (<a href="/wiki/Diophantine_geometry" title="Diophantine geometry">Diophantine geometry</a>). Questions in number theory are often best understood through the study of <a href="/wiki/Complex_analysis" title="Complex analysis">analytical</a> objects (for example, the <a href="/wiki/Riemann_zeta_function" title="Riemann zeta function">Riemann zeta function</a>) that encode properties of the integers, primes or other number-theoretic objects in some fashion (<a href="/wiki/Analytic_number_theory" title="Analytic number theory">analytic number theory</a>). One may also study <a href="/wiki/Real_number" title="Real number">real numbers</a> in relation to rational numbers, for example, as approximated by the latter (<a href="/wiki/Diophantine_approximation" title="Diophantine approximation">Diophantine approximation</a>).
</p><p>The older term for number theory is <i>arithmetic</i>. By the early twentieth century, it had been superseded by "number theory".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[note 2]</a></sup> (The word "<a href="/wiki/Arithmetic" title="Arithmetic">arithmetic</a>" is used by the general public to mean "<a href="/wiki/Elementary_arithmetic" title="Elementary arithmetic">elementary calculations</a>"; it has also acquired other meanings in <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_logic" title="Mathematical logic">mathematical logic</a>, as in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peano_arithmetic" title="Peano arithmetic">Peano arithmetic</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>, as in <i><a href="/wiki/Floating-point_arithmetic" title="Floating-point arithmetic">floating-point arithmetic</a></i>.) The use of the term <i>arithmetic</i> for <i>number theory</i> regained some ground in the second half of the 20th century, arguably in part due to French influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[note 3]</a></sup> In particular, <i>arithmetical</i> is commonly preferred as an adjective to <i>number-theoretic</i>.
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Other_subfields"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Other subfields</span></a>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Number_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Number_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dawn_of_arithmetic">Dawn of arithmetic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Number_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Dawn of arithmetic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plimpton_322.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="863" data-file-width="1246" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Plimpton_322.jpg/220px-Plimpton_322.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Plimpton_322.jpg/330px-Plimpton_322.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Plimpton_322.jpg/440px-Plimpton_322.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plimpton_322.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Plimpton 322 tablet</div></div></div>
<p>The earliest historical find of an arithmetical nature is a fragment of a table: the broken clay tablet <a href="/wiki/Plimpton_322" title="Plimpton 322">Plimpton 322</a> (<a href="/wiki/Larsa" title="Larsa">Larsa, Mesopotamia</a>, ca. 1800 BC) contains a list of "<a href="/wiki/Pythagorean_triple" title="Pythagorean triple">Pythagorean triples</a>", that is, integers <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle (a,b,c)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>a</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>b</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>c</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle (a,b,c)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="(a,b,c)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ae973a762a92b9cd3eafe7f283890ccfa9b887e8" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:7.111ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> such that <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle a^{2}+b^{2}=c^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>+</mo>
<msup>
<mi>b</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>=</mo>
<msup>
<mi>c</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle a^{2}+b^{2}=c^{2}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="a^{2}+b^{2}=c^{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7ef0a5a4b8ab98870ae5d6d7c7b4dfe3fb6612e2" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:12.336ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>.
The triples are too many and too large to have been obtained by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brute_force_method" title="Brute force method">brute force</a>. The heading over the first column reads: "The <i>takiltum</i> of the diagonal which has been subtracted such that the width..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>The table's layout suggests<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[3]</a></sup> that it was constructed by means of what amounts, in modern language, to the <a href="/wiki/Identity_(mathematics)" title="Identity (mathematics)">identity</a>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \left({\frac {1}{2}}\left(x-{\frac {1}{x}}\right)\right)^{2}+1=\left({\frac {1}{2}}\left(x+{\frac {1}{x}}\right)\right)^{2},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mrow>
<mo>(</mo>
<mrow>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mi>x</mi>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mo>)</mo>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mo>)</mo>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \left({\frac {1}{2}}\left(x-{\frac {1}{x}}\right)\right)^{2}+1=\left({\frac {1}{2}}\left(x+{\frac {1}{x}}\right)\right)^{2},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \left({\frac {1}{2}}\left(x-{\frac {1}{x}}\right)\right)^{2}+1=\left({\frac {1}{2}}\left(x+{\frac {1}{x}}\right)\right)^{2},}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e9be9111eb813cf2c8b31c740ddff47308c1e1a3" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:40.984ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>which is implicit in routine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Babylonian_language" title="Old Babylonian language">Old Babylonian</a> exercises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Waerden1961184_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Waerden1961184-7">[4]</a></sup> If some other method was used,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[5]</a></sup> the triples were first constructed and then reordered by <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle c/a}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>c</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>/</mo>
</mrow>
<mi>a</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle c/a}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="c/a" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/11e52b3c6d3960cfc6efa96a811ec2feb5efa2b4" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:3.399ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>, presumably for actual use as a "table", for example, with a view to applications.
</p><p>It is not known what these applications may have been, or whether there could have been any; <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_astronomy" title="Babylonian astronomy">Babylonian astronomy</a>, for example, truly came into its own only later. It has been suggested instead that the table was a source of numerical examples for school problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFriberg1981302_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFriberg1981302-9">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[note 4]</a></sup>
</p><p>While Babylonian number theory—or what survives of <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_mathematics" title="Babylonian mathematics">Babylonian mathematics</a> that can be called thus—consists of this single, striking fragment, Babylonian algebra (in the secondary-school sense of "<a href="/wiki/Algebra" title="Algebra">algebra</a>") was exceptionally well developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Waerden196143_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Waerden196143-11">[7]</a></sup> Late Neoplatonic sources<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vanderW2_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-vanderW2-12">[8]</a></sup> state that <a href="/wiki/Pythagoras" title="Pythagoras">Pythagoras</a> learned mathematics from the Babylonians. Much earlier sources<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanencyc_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-stanencyc-13">[9]</a></sup> state that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thales" title="Thales">Thales</a> and Pythagoras traveled and studied in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a> IX 21–34 is very probably Pythagorean;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Becker_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Becker-14">[10]</a></sup> it is very simple material ("odd times even is even", "if an odd number measures [= divides] an even number, then it also measures [= divides] half of it"), but it is all that is needed to prove that <a href="/wiki/Square_root_of_2" title="Square root of 2"><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\sqrt {2}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msqrt>
<mn>2</mn>
</msqrt>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\sqrt {2}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\sqrt {2}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b4afc1e27d418021bf10898eb44a7f5f315735ff" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:3.098ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span></a>
is <a href="/wiki/Irrational_number" title="Irrational number">irrational</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBecker1936_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBecker1936-15">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pythagoreanism" title="Pythagoreanism">Pythagorean mystics</a> gave great importance to the odd and the even.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Waerden1961109_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Waerden1961109-16">[12]</a></sup>
The discovery that <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\sqrt {2}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msqrt>
<mn>2</mn>
</msqrt>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\sqrt {2}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\sqrt {2}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b4afc1e27d418021bf10898eb44a7f5f315735ff" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:3.098ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span> is irrational is credited to the early Pythagoreans (pre-<a href="/wiki/Theodorus_of_Cyrene" title="Theodorus of Cyrene">Theodorus</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thea_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thea-17">[13]</a></sup> By revealing (in modern terms) that numbers could be irrational, this discovery seems to have provoked the first foundational crisis in mathematical history; its proof or its divulgation are sometimes credited to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hippasus_of_Metapontum" title="Hippasus of Metapontum">Hippasus</a>, who was expelled or split from the Pythagorean sect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Fritz2004_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Fritz2004-18">[14]</a></sup> This forced a distinction between <i>numbers</i> (integers and the rationals—the subjects of arithmetic), on the one hand, and <i>lengths</i> and <i>proportions</i> (which we would identify with real numbers, whether rational or not), on the other hand.
</p><p>The Pythagorean tradition spoke also of so-called <a href="/wiki/Polygonal_number" title="Polygonal number">polygonal</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Figurate_numbers" title="Figurate numbers">figurate numbers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeath192176_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeath192176-19">[15]</a></sup> While <a href="/wiki/Square_number" title="Square number">square numbers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cubic_number" title="Cubic number">cubic numbers</a>, etc., are seen now as more natural than <a href="/wiki/Triangular_number" title="Triangular number">triangular numbers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pentagonal_number" title="Pentagonal number">pentagonal numbers</a>, etc., the study of the sums of triangular and pentagonal numbers would prove fruitful in the early modern period (17th to early 19th century).
</p><p>We know of no clearly arithmetical material in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egyptian_mathematics" title="Egyptian mathematics">ancient Egyptian</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vedic" title="Vedic">Vedic</a> sources, though there is some algebra in each. The <a href="/wiki/Chinese_remainder_theorem" title="Chinese remainder theorem">Chinese remainder theorem</a> appears as an exercise <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[16]</a></sup> in <i><a href="/wiki/Sunzi_Suanjing" title="Sunzi Suanjing">Sunzi Suanjing</a></i> (3rd, 4th or 5th century CE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-YongSe_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-YongSe-21">[17]</a></sup> (There is one important step glossed over in Sunzi's solution:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[note 5]</a></sup> it is the problem that was later solved by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C4%80ryabha%E1%B9%ADa" title="Āryabhaṭa">Āryabhaṭa</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Ku%E1%B9%AD%E1%B9%ADaka" title="Kuṭṭaka">Kuṭṭaka</a> – see <a href="#Āryabhaṭa,_Brahmagupta,_Bhāskara">below</a>.)
</p><p>There is also some numerical mysticism in Chinese mathematics,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[note 6]</a></sup> but, unlike that of the Pythagoreans, it seems to have led nowhere. Like the Pythagoreans' <a href="/wiki/Perfect_number" title="Perfect number">perfect numbers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magic_squares" title="Magic squares">magic squares</a> have passed from superstition into <a href="/wiki/Recreational_mathematics" title="Recreational mathematics">recreation</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_Greece_and_the_early_Hellenistic_period">Classical Greece and the early Hellenistic period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Number_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Classical Greece and the early Hellenistic period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_mathematics" title="Ancient Greek mathematics">Ancient Greek mathematics</a></div>
<p>Aside from a few fragments, the mathematics of Classical Greece is known to us either through the reports of contemporary non-mathematicians or through mathematical works from the early Hellenistic period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyerMerzbach199182_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyerMerzbach199182-24">[18]</a></sup> In the case of number theory, this means, by and large, <i>Plato</i> and <i>Euclid</i>, respectively.
</p><p>While Asian mathematics influenced Greek and Hellenistic learning, it seems to be the case that Greek mathematics is also an indigenous tradition.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Eusebius" title="Eusebius">Eusebius</a>, PE X, chapter 4 mentions of <a href="/wiki/Pythagoras" title="Pythagoras">Pythagoras</a>:
</p>
<blockquote><p>"In fact the said Pythagoras, while busily studying the wisdom of each nation, visited Babylon, and Egypt, and all Persia, being instructed by the Magi and the priests: and in addition to these he is related to have studied under the Brahmans (these are Indian philosophers); and from some he gathered astrology, from others geometry, and arithmetic and music from others, and different things from different nations, and only from the wise men of Greece did he get nothing, wedded as they were to a poverty and dearth of wisdom: so on the contrary he himself became the author of instruction to the Greeks in the learning which he had procured from abroad."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[19]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Aristotle claimed that the philosophy of Plato closely followed the teachings of the Pythagoreans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[20]</a></sup> and Cicero repeats this claim: <i>Platonem ferunt didicisse Pythagorea omnia</i> ("They say Plato learned all things Pythagorean").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> had a keen interest in mathematics, and distinguished clearly between arithmetic and calculation. (By <i>arithmetic</i> he meant, in part, theorising on number, rather than what <i>arithmetic</i> or <i>number theory</i> have come to mean.) It is through one of Plato's dialogues—namely, <a href="/wiki/Theaetetus_(dialogue)" title="Theaetetus (dialogue)"><i>Theaetetus</i></a>—that we know that <a href="/wiki/Theodorus_of_Cyrene" title="Theodorus of Cyrene">Theodorus</a> had proven that <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\sqrt {3}},{\sqrt {5}},\dots ,{\sqrt {17}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msqrt>
<mn>3</mn>
</msqrt>
</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msqrt>
<mn>5</mn>
</msqrt>
</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
<mo>…<!-- … --></mo>
<mo>,</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msqrt>
<mn>17</mn>
</msqrt>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\sqrt {3}},{\sqrt {5}},\dots ,{\sqrt {17}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\sqrt  {3}},{\sqrt  {5}},\dots ,{\sqrt  {17}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4c30069c6e8ec13aa767b2a0fe071bba15bedcc2" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:16.67ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span> are irrational. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theaetetus_of_Athens" title="Theaetetus of Athens">Theaetetus</a> was, like Plato, a disciple of Theodorus's; he worked on distinguishing different kinds of <a href="/wiki/Commensurability_(mathematics)" title="Commensurability (mathematics)">incommensurables</a>, and was thus arguably a pioneer in the study of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Number_systems" title="Number systems">number systems</a>. (Book X of <a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_Elements" title="Euclid's Elements">Euclid's Elements</a> is described by <a href="/wiki/Pappus_of_Alexandria" title="Pappus of Alexandria">Pappus</a> as being largely based on Theaetetus's work.)
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a> devoted part of his <i>Elements</i> to prime numbers and divisibility, topics that belong unambiguously to number theory and are basic to it (Books VII to IX of <a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_Elements" title="Euclid's Elements">Euclid's Elements</a>). In particular, he gave an algorithm for computing the greatest common divisor of two numbers (the <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_algorithm" title="Euclidean algorithm">Euclidean algorithm</a>; <i>Elements</i>, Prop. VII.2) and the first known proof of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infinitude_of_primes" title="Infinitude of primes">infinitude of primes</a> (<i>Elements</i>, Prop. IX.20).
</p><p>In 1773, <a href="/wiki/Gotthold_Ephraim_Lessing" title="Gotthold Ephraim Lessing">Lessing</a> published an <a href="/wiki/Epigram" title="Epigram">epigram</a> he had found in a manuscript during his work as a librarian; it claimed to be a letter sent by <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a> to <a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">Eratosthenes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVardi1998305–19_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVardi1998305–19-28">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeil198417–24_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeil198417–24-29">[23]</a></sup> The epigram proposed what has become known as
<a href="/wiki/Archimedes%27s_cattle_problem" title="Archimedes's cattle problem">Archimedes's cattle problem</a>; its solution (absent from the manuscript) requires solving an indeterminate quadratic equation (which reduces to what would later be misnamed <a href="/wiki/Pell%27s_equation" title="Pell's equation">Pell's equation</a>). As far as we know, such equations were first successfully treated by the <a href="#Āryabhaṭa,_Brahmagupta,_Bhāskara">Indian school</a>. It is not known whether Archimedes himself had a method of solution.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Diophantus">Diophantus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Number_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Diophantus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diophantus-cover.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="591" data-file-width="360" decoding="async" height="279" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Diophantus-cover.jpg/170px-Diophantus-cover.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Diophantus-cover.jpg/255px-Diophantus-cover.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Diophantus-cover.jpg/340px-Diophantus-cover.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diophantus-cover.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Title page of the 1621 edition of Diophantus's <i>Arithmetica</i>, translated into <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Claude_Gaspard_Bachet_de_M%C3%A9ziriac" title="Claude Gaspard Bachet de Méziriac">Claude Gaspard Bachet de Méziriac</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Very little is known about <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diophantus_of_Alexandria" title="Diophantus of Alexandria">Diophantus of Alexandria</a>; he probably lived in the third century AD, that is, about five hundred years after Euclid. Six out of the thirteen books of Diophantus's <i><a href="/wiki/Arithmetica" title="Arithmetica">Arithmetica</a></i> survive in the original Greek and four more survive in an Arabic translation. The <i>Arithmetica</i> is a collection of worked-out problems where the task is invariably to find rational solutions to a system of polynomial equations, usually of the form <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle f(x,y)=z^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<msup>

</math></span><img alt="f(x,y,z)=w^{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1004a1f588de9d793e07ee911e4378555096b606" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:14.546ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span>. Thus, nowadays, we speak of <i>Diophantine equations</i> when we speak of polynomial equations to which rational or integer solutions must be found.
</p><p>One may say that Diophantus was studying rational points, that is, points whose coordinates are rational—on <a href="/wiki/Curve" title="Curve">curves</a> and <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_variety" title="Algebraic variety">algebraic varieties</a>; however, unlike the Greeks of the Classical period, who did what we would now call basic algebra in geometrical terms, Diophantus did what we would now call basic algebraic geometry in purely algebraic terms. In modern language, what Diophantus did was to find rational parametrizations of varieties; that is, given an equation of the form (say)

</p><p>Diophantus also studied the equations of some non-rational curves, for which no rational parametrisation is possible. He managed to find some rational points on these curves (<a href="/wiki/Elliptic_curve" title="Elliptic curve">elliptic curves</a>, as it happens, in what seems to be their first known occurrence) by means of what amounts to a tangent construction: translated into coordinate geometry
(which did not exist in Diophantus's time), his method would be visualised as drawing a tangent to a curve at a known rational point, and then finding the other point of intersection of the tangent with the curve; that other point is a new rational point. (Diophantus also resorted to what could be called a special case of a secant construction.)
</p><p>While Diophantus was concerned largely with rational solutions, he assumed some results on integer numbers, in particular that <a href="/wiki/Lagrange%27s_four-square_theorem" title="Lagrange's four-square theorem">every integer is the sum of four squares</a> (though he never stated as much explicitly).
</p>
<h4><span id=".C4.80ryabha.E1.B9.ADa.2C_Brahmagupta.2C_Bh.C4.81skara"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Āryabhaṭa,_Brahmagupta,_Bhāskara">Āryabhaṭa, Brahmagupta, Bhāskara</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Number_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Āryabhaṭa, Brahmagupta, Bhāskara">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>While Greek astronomy probably influenced Indian learning, to the point of introducing trigonometry,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlofker2008119_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlofker2008119-30">[24]</a></sup> it seems to be the case that Indian mathematics is otherwise an indigenous tradition;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plofbab_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plofbab-31">[25]</a></sup> in particular, there is no evidence that Euclid's Elements reached India before the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMumford2010387_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMumford2010387-32">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Aryabhata" title="Aryabhata">Āryabhaṭa</a> (476–550 AD) showed that pairs of simultaneous congruences <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle n\equiv a_{1}{\bmod {m}}_{1}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>n</mi>
<mo>≡<!-- ≡ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>a</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<msub>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo lspace="thickmathspace" rspace="thickmathspace">mod</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>m</mi>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle n\equiv a_{1}{\bmod {m}}_{1}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle n\equiv a_{1}{\bmod {m}}_{1}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c663611bcfb301bc7826f1b93ccd106dec042744" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:15.553ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle n\equiv a_{2}{\bmod {m}}_{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<mo>≡<!-- ≡ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>a</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<msub>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo lspace="thickmathspace" rspace="thickmathspace">mod</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>m</mi>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle n\equiv a_{2}{\bmod {m}}_{2}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle n\equiv a_{2}{\bmod {m}}_{2}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/70c47219ea418a20f473f14a09b48959cbe71125" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:15.553ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> could be solved by a method he called <i>kuṭṭaka</i>, or <i>pulveriser</i>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[27]</a></sup> this is a procedure close to (a generalisation of) the <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_algorithm" title="Euclidean algorithm">Euclidean algorithm</a>, which was probably discovered independently in India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMumford2010388_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMumford2010388-34">[28]</a></sup> Āryabhaṭa seems to have had in mind applications to astronomical calculations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlofker2008119_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlofker2008119-30">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Brahmagupta" title="Brahmagupta">Brahmagupta</a> (628 AD) started the systematic study of indefinite quadratic equations—in particular, the misnamed <a href="/wiki/Pell%27s_equation" title="Pell's equation">Pell equation</a>, in which <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a> may have first been interested, and which did not start to be solved in the West until the time of Fermat and Euler. Later Sanskrit authors would follow, using Brahmagupta's technical terminology. A general procedure (the <a href="/wiki/Chakravala_method" title="Chakravala method">chakravala</a>, or "cyclic method") for solving Pell's equation was finally found by Jayadeva (cited in the eleventh century; his work is otherwise lost); the earliest surviving exposition appears in <a href="/wiki/Bh%C4%81skara_II" title="Bhāskara II">Bhāskara II</a>'s Bīja-gaṇita (twelfth century).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlofker2008194_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlofker2008194-35">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Indian mathematics remained largely unknown in Europe until the late eighteenth century;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlofker2008283_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlofker2008283-36">[30]</a></sup> Brahmagupta and Bhāskara's work was translated into English in 1817 by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Thomas_Colebrooke" title="Henry Thomas Colebrooke">Henry Colebrooke</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEColebrooke1817_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEColebrooke1817-37">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Arithmetic_in_the_Islamic_golden_age">Arithmetic in the Islamic golden age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Number_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Arithmetic in the Islamic golden age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mathematics_in_medieval_Islam" title="Mathematics in medieval Islam">Mathematics in medieval Islam</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hevelius_Selenographia_frontispiece.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1456" data-file-width="864" decoding="async" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Hevelius_Selenographia_frontispiece.png/170px-Hevelius_Selenographia_frontispiece.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Hevelius_Selenographia_frontispiece.png/255px-Hevelius_Selenographia_frontispiece.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Hevelius_Selenographia_frontispiece.png/340px-Hevelius_Selenographia_frontispiece.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hevelius_Selenographia_frontispiece.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Haytham" title="Al-Haytham">Al-Haytham</a> as seen by the West: on the frontispiece of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selenographia" title="Selenographia">Selenographia</a></i> Alhasen  [<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>] represents knowledge through reason and Galileo knowledge through the senses.</div></div></div>
<p>In the early ninth century, the caliph <a href="/wiki/Al-Ma%27mun" title="Al-Ma'mun">Al-Ma'mun</a> ordered translations of many Greek mathematical works and at least one Sanskrit work (the <i>Sindhind</i>,
which may <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[32]</a></sup> or may not<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plofnot_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plofnot-39">[33]</a></sup> be <a href="/wiki/Brahmagupta" title="Brahmagupta">Brahmagupta</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahmasphutasiddhanta" title="Brahmasphutasiddhanta">Brāhmasphuṭasiddhānta</a>).
Diophantus's main work, the <i>Arithmetica</i>, was translated into Arabic by <a href="/wiki/Qusta_ibn_Luqa" title="Qusta ibn Luqa">Qusta ibn Luqa</a> (820–912).
Part of the treatise <i>al-Fakhri</i> (by <a href="/wiki/Al-Karaji" title="Al-Karaji">al-Karajī</a>, 953 – ca. 1029) builds on it to some extent. According to Rashed Roshdi, Al-Karajī's contemporary <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Haytham" title="Ibn al-Haytham">Ibn al-Haytham</a> knew<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERashed1980305–21_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERashed1980305–21-40">[34]</a></sup> what would later be called <a href="/wiki/Wilson%27s_theorem" title="Wilson's theorem">Wilson's theorem</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_Europe_in_the_Middle_Ages">Western Europe in the Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Number_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Western Europe in the Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Other than a treatise on squares in arithmetic progression by <a href="/wiki/Fibonacci" title="Fibonacci">Fibonacci</a>—who traveled and studied in north Africa and Constantinople—no number theory to speak of was done in western Europe during the Middle Ages. Matters started to change in Europe in the late <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, thanks to a renewed study of the works of Greek antiquity. A catalyst was the textual emendation and translation into Latin of Diophantus' <i><a href="/wiki/Arithmetica" title="Arithmetica">Arithmetica</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_modern_number_theory">Early modern number theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Number_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Early modern number theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fermat">Fermat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Number_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Fermat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pierre_de_Fermat.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1003" data-file-width="566" decoding="async" height="301" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Pierre_de_Fermat.png/170px-Pierre_de_Fermat.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Pierre_de_Fermat.png/255px-Pierre_de_Fermat.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Pierre_de_Fermat.png/340px-Pierre_de_Fermat.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pierre_de_Fermat.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pierre_de_Fermat" title="Pierre de Fermat">Pierre de Fermat</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pierre_de_Fermat" title="Pierre de Fermat">Pierre de Fermat</a> (1607–1665) never published his writings; in particular, his work on number theory is contained almost entirely in letters to mathematicians and in private marginal notes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeil198445–46_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeil198445–46-42">[36]</a></sup> In his notes and letters, he scarcely wrote any proofs - he had no models in the area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Over his lifetime, Fermat made the following contributions to the field:
</p>
<ul><li>One of Fermat's first interests was <a href="/wiki/Perfect_number" title="Perfect number">perfect numbers</a> (which appear in Euclid, <i>Elements</i> IX) and <a href="/wiki/Amicable_numbers" title="Amicable numbers">amicable numbers</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[note 7]</a></sup> these topics led him to work on integer <a href="/wiki/Divisor" title="Divisor">divisors</a>, which were from the beginning among the subjects of the correspondence (1636 onwards) that put him in touch with the mathematical community of the day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[38]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1638, Fermat claimed, without proof, that all whole numbers can be expressed as the sum of four squares or fewer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[39]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fermat%27s_little_theorem" title="Fermat's little theorem">Fermat's little theorem</a> (1640):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[40]</a></sup> if <i>a</i> is not divisible by a prime <i>p</i>, then <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle a^{p-1}\equiv 1{\bmod {p}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle a^{p-1}\equiv 1{\bmod {p}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle a^{p-1}\equiv 1{\bmod {p}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/24ec1de006b31585777b0bd54505db183dc0281e" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:16.148ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[note 8]</a></sup></li>
<li>If <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are coprime, then <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle a^{2}+b^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="a^2 + b^2" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/14fc28103c5d2aa9276728469f82c9f415f4b257" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:7.176ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETanneryHenry1891Vol._II,_p._213_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETanneryHenry1891Vol._II,_p._213-50">[42]</a></sup> These two statements also date from 1640; in 1659, Fermat stated to Huygens that he had proven the latter statement by the <a href="/wiki/Proof_by_infinite_descent" title="Proof by infinite descent">method of infinite descent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETanneryHenry1891Vol._II,_p._423_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETanneryHenry1891Vol._II,_p._423-51">[43]</a></sup></li>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Euler">Euler</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Number_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Euler">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leonhard_Euler.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6040" data-file-width="4672" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Leonhard_Euler.jpg/170px-Leonhard_Euler.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Leonhard_Euler.jpg/255px-Leonhard_Euler.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Leonhard_Euler.jpg/340px-Leonhard_Euler.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leonhard_Euler.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Leonhard_Euler" title="Leonhard Euler">Leonhard Euler</a></div></div></div>
<p>The interest of <a href="/wiki/Leonhard_Euler" title="Leonhard Euler">Leonhard Euler</a> (1707–1783) in number theory was first spurred in 1729, when a friend of his, the amateur<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[note 9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christian_Goldbach" title="Christian Goldbach">Goldbach</a>, pointed him towards some of Fermat's work on the subject.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeil19842,_172_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeil19842,_172-57">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVaradarajan20069_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVaradarajan20069-58">[49]</a></sup> This has been called the "rebirth" of modern number theory,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeil19841–2_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeil19841–2-59">[50]</a></sup> after Fermat's relative lack of success in getting his contemporaries' attention for the subject.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[51]</a></sup> Euler's work on number theory includes the following:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i>Proofs for Fermat's statements.</i> This includes <a href="/wiki/Fermat%27s_little_theorem" title="Fermat's little theorem">Fermat's little theorem</a> (generalised by Euler to non-prime moduli); the fact that <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle p=x^{2}+y^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>p</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<msup>
<mi>x</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>+</mo>
<msup>
<mi>y</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle p=x^{2}+y^{2}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="p=x^{2}+y^{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fe433e9ba9c42efe1bc9fd51bb439c698b8f6843" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; margin-left: -0.089ex; width:11.796ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span> if and only if <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle p\equiv 1{\bmod {4}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
